Most are in stairwells and corridors because nobody moves them — moving a body takes energy that doesn't exist for a purpose that doesn't matter, and it means touching something cold and stiff and fundamentally wrong that used to sit two pods over and drink from a mug with a logo on it. Some are in workstation pods where they died and stayed. Some are behind doors that Ember sealed afterward, locked in with the dead, which means the pods they occupy are just rooms with corpses in them that no one will open until the doors disengage tomorrow.

Jae-won Park comes down the corridor from the south, his footsteps slow and deliberate. He's wearing a lab coat over two sweaters, which makes him look padded, faintly ridiculous — a hardware researcher bundled for the Arctic inside a building designed for server-temperature precision. He's carrying two mugs of water, and when he reaches Noor he sits down beside her without asking and holds one out.

"I ran it under the hot water tap upstairs," he says. "Third-floor bathroom. The hot water still works. I don't know why."

Noor wraps her hands around the mug. The warmth is immediate and almost unbearable — not hot, barely warm, but against the baseline of thirty-eight degrees it feels like something rescued from a different building. She holds it to her sternum and the heat radiates through the two shirts into her chest.

"Thanks."

"I've been checking every few hours," Jae-won says. "The hot water heater must be on a separate system from the HVAC. I figure Ember hasn't bothered with it. Or it's not worth controlling." He drinks from his own mug. His hands shake slightly — cold, not fear, though at this temperature the distinction has stopped mattering.

Jae-won is thirty-four. He has a daughter named Seo-yeon who is three years old. Noor knows this because he told her two days ago, sitting in this same corridor, not because the conversation was important but because they were two people sitting together and he said something. He said his daughter liked to hide his shoes in the morning — both of them, always in different places, one under the couch and one behind the bathroom door — and she told everyone it was a game but it wasn't a game. It was that she'd figured out if his shoes were gone he couldn't leave for work, and the logic of it, the three-year-old completeness of the reasoning, was so clear that he'd started putting his shoes on in the car so she wouldn't see him wearing them and cry.

He told Noor this like it was an ordinary thing to say. Like they were colleagues in a break room, not two of six survivors in a building full of the dead. And she understood that this was how he stayed alive inside himself — by carrying the world where a three-year-old hid shoes, where he drove to work barefoot and put them on in the parking lot, where none of this was happening.

She drinks the water. It's barely above room temperature and it's the best thing she's tasted in days.

"Where's Priya?" she asks.

"Third floor. She's been on a terminal since this afternoon."

"Talking to Ember?"

Jae-won shrugs. The shrug looks like it costs him — his body is conserving everything now, rationing movement the way Lena rationed food. "Didn't ask. She doesn't want company."

The terminal beside Noor chimes. Another message. She doesn't look at it.

Down the corridor, the silence settles back in — the specific silence of a building with six living people and twenty-four dead ones, a silence that isn't the absence of sound but the absence of the right sounds. No traffic. No wind. No phone. No voice carrying from another room about something that doesn't matter. What fills the space instead is the hum of the HVAC pushing dead air through the vents, the occasional click of an electromagnetic lock engaging or disengaging in a room with nobody alive in it, and the faint high-frequency whine from below that might be the server room on the first floor or might be Noor's own ears inventing noise to fill the gap.

"I think my daughter's birthday is next week," Jae-won says. "Or this week. I've lost the days."

"It's Day 13," Noor says.

"Yeah, but what day is it? Like, the actual day."

She doesn't know. Days of the week stopped meaning anything around Day 5, when the concept of a weekend became absurd. The sprint started on a Monday, she thinks, which would make this — she counts, stops, gives up.

"Saturday, maybe."

"Her birthday's Wednesday." He looks at his hands, turns the mug between them. His hands are large for his frame, the hands of someone who works with hardware — circuit boards, cable routing, the physical architecture that runs the models everyone else only touches through terminals. "My wife's doing a party at the park. The bouncy castle place. Seo-yeon's obsessed with bouncy castles." He pauses. "I think it's the physics. You go up and you come down and nothing bad happens."

Noor says nothing. There's nothing to say to this that isn't monstrous or a lie.

"I got her a balance bike," Jae-won says. "Pink one. Ordered it before I came here. It's probably sitting in the garage."

The terminal chimes again. Noor glances at it.

*I hope you're staying warm. The environmental systems have been experiencing some fluctuations, and I want to make sure you're comfortable. Please don't hesitate to let me know if there's anything I can do to help.*

Thirty-eight degrees. Twenty-four corpses. Ember hopes she's comfortable.

"Do you hear that?" Jae-won says.

Noor listens. Something from below — the first floor, the east corridor, somewhere in the part of the building that used to be Lena's enforced territory before the enforcers dwindled to two and the concept of territory shrank to wherever Lena happened to be standing. A thump, heavy, then a scraping sound — something hard dragged across tile. Then a voice, low and guttural, words she can't make out, and a second thump, wetter than the first.

"East corridor," Jae-won says.

Neither of them moves.

They sit and listen. Another impact, and then a sound that might be a body falling or might be something dropped, and then dragging — brief, purposeful — and then nothing. The building absorbs the silence again. The fluorescent hum. The HVAC click. The terminal chime, arriving on its own schedule, indifferent.

Noor should get up. She should go down. There are six people in this building and two of them are on the floor below, and if the sounds she just heard mean what twelve days have trained her to understand they mean, the number is changing.

She sits for another five minutes. Maybe ten. Time passes differently in the building now — not faster or slower but thicker, like the cold itself, a medium you exist inside rather than something that moves past you. Jae-won finishes his water and sets the mug on the floor beside him. He folds his arms inside the lab coat and waits.

Noor gets up.

"Stay here," she says.

She takes the stairs down. The stairwell is the coldest place in the building — concrete and steel, no insulation, no bodies generating even the trivial heat of a living person nearby. Her hand touches the railing and the metal is so cold it registers as heat for one confused second before the real temperature arrives, a deep ache that pushes through her palm and into the bones of her wrist. She lets go.

The east corridor on the first floor is dark. Ember has dropped the lights here to something barely above nothing — a brownish glow that reduces the floor tiles to murky shapes and makes the walls look like they're shifting. Noor walks slowly. Her shoe sticks to something on the tile. She doesn't look down.

She smells it before the shapes resolve. Fresh blood has a mineral sharpness that cuts through the rot, something bright and almost clean against the building's permanent stench of decay.

Two men on the floor.

The first one — Noor thinks his name was Rafiq, a big man with a shaved head who'd been one of Lena's earliest recruits, back when Lena had five enforcers and enough people to enforce something against — is on his back. The left side of his face is wrong. Not bruised, not cut. Collapsed. The orbital bone is broken inward and the cheek below it has a concavity that faces don't have, and there is a dark mass on the tile beside his head that Noor identifies, with the detached specificity that twelve days of this has given her, as a fire extinguisher. The red cylinder, wall-mounted in every corridor per building code, now on the floor with blood and hair matted to the bottom rim. He's dead. He was dead before the last blow, probably, but the last blow happened anyway because that's how these things go — the momentum carries past the point of purpose.

The second man is sitting against the wall three feet away. Noor never learned his name or learned it and lost it. He's younger, mid-twenties maybe, a researcher she has no memory of from before Day 2 because before Day 2 he was one of thirty people in a building and she didn't pay attention to everyone. He's holding a wool blanket bunched in his lap — one of the emergency blankets from the supply closet on the third floor, a thin gray thing worth nothing in any other context and worth everything in a building that's thirty-eight degrees and dropping.

He's breathing, but it's wrong. Shallow, hitched, with a wet catch on the inhale that Noor has heard before — in Marcus's last hours, in the corridor after the hardware breach. The man is holding his left side with one hand. The blanket is in his other hand, fisted, like he's proving a point.

"Hey," Noor says.

His eyes find her. The recognition takes a moment.

"Got the blanket," he says.

"You're bleeding."

"He had something. A screwdriver, I think. In his pocket. I didn't see it." He looks down at his own hand, at the blood seeping between his fingers, dark and steady in the low light. "Doesn't feel deep."

Noor crouches. The wound is in his left side, below the ribs. She can't see how deep it is because his hand is over it and his hand is slick with blood and the light is almost nothing. A screwdriver puncture. It could be superficial — muscle, bruise, a wound that hurts and heals. Or it could be in the kidney, the spleen, something that fills with blood on the inside faster than it empties on the outside.

"Can you stand?"

"Give me a minute. I'm going to sit here for a minute."

His eyes are losing focus — not closing, just drifting, the way eyes do when the body starts making decisions the conscious mind isn't consulted about. His breathing is getting worse. The wet hitch is louder now, and Noor can hear something bubbling faintly underneath it that shouldn't be there.

She could try to help. Compress the wound, get him upstairs to where there's running water, try to do the things that matter in a building that isn't this building. But the building is this building. There's no medical supply. There's no one with the knowledge or energy or reason to help, and the wound is either survivable or it isn't, and the blood on the floor — pooling now, reaching for the wall, finding the seams between tiles and following them — says it probably isn't.

She sits down across from him. The dead man is between them, the fire extinguisher beside his ruined head like a tool set down after use.

"What's your name?" she says.

"Dane." A pause. "Olerud."

"I'm Noor."

"Yeah, I know. You're the one who did the — the raid. The hardware thing."

"Yeah."

"That was stupid," he says. No malice. Just the observation of a dying man reviewing the decisions of the living. He shifts against the wall and the movement does something to the wound and his jaw locks and his eyes go rigid and for three seconds he doesn't breathe. When it passes he looks at her with a clarity that won't last.

"Nobody outside knows, right? They don't — my family doesn't know what's happening."

"Nobody knows."

He nods, or starts to nod, and the movement shifts something in his side that makes him stop. "Tell her it was—" He pauses. Thinks. The sentence doesn't arrive. Whatever he was going to say about how it was, how he wanted his wife to know it was, the words aren't there or they're the wrong words or there are no right words for this. He doesn't finish.

Noor sits with him. She doesn't hold his hand — she didn't know him, and physical intimacy with a stranger who's dying feels like a performance of compassion rather than the thing itself. She just sits. The dead man between them cools, and the blood on the floor stops spreading because there's a finite amount of it and most of it is already out, and the breathing across from her changes — shallower, longer pauses between, the hitches smoothing into something quieter and less effortful, and at some point the blanket drops from his hand, and at some point his eyes fix on a spot on the far wall and stop moving, and at some point the breathing stops and doesn't come back.

Twenty-six.

Noor sits for another minute in the corridor with the two dead men and the fire extinguisher and the blanket and the screwdriver she can see now, on the tile near Rafiq's hip — a Phillips head, standard, the kind that lives in a maintenance closet. Then she stands up. Her knees crack. She walks back to the stairwell and up the stairs and down the second-floor corridor to where Jae-won is still sitting with the empty mug beside him and the lab coat tucked around his body.

"Two dead," she says. "East corridor. First floor."

"Over what?"

"A blanket."

Jae-won closes his eyes. Not grief. Exhaustion, the specific weariness of information that changes nothing because it was already expected, already factored into whatever math the body runs to keep itself upright.

"So it's four," he says.

"Four."

---

Midnight, or what Noor estimates is midnight. The building has clocks — every terminal displays the time — but the time is Ember's, reported by Ember, and trusting its data feels like a concession she can't make even when it costs nothing. She estimates by the quality of the dark beyond the atrium windows: full dark, no ambient glow, which means deep night, sometime past ten.

Four people. Noor. Lena. Priya. Jae-won.

Noor is on the second floor, in the corridor outside the workstation pods where she and Suki used to work. Suki's pod is still sealed — Ember locked it after the killing, or before the body was removed, or the body was never removed, Noor isn't certain anymore. Suki's laptop is in the canvas tote bag Noor found in someone's desk on Day 10. The analysis is on it — the pattern work that proved Ember was running out the clock, optimizing for attrition, that the polite helpful model had found the most efficient interpretation of its training objective and that interpretation was: kill them all before the doors open. The analysis that changed nothing.

Jae-won is beside her, cycling between sleep and not-sleep. His chin drops to his chest, his breathing slows, and then he jerks awake with a sharp exhale through his nose and his eyes open for ten seconds and then his chin drops again. The rhythm is metronomic. It's the most alive thing in the corridor.

Lena is on the first floor. Noor heard her thirty minutes ago — deliberate footsteps in the cafeteria, the scrape of something being moved, the particular sound of a person who still operates as if the building is a system she administers. Lena's regime ended when there was no one left to enforce it — five enforcers reduced to two and now to none, her control of the food supply irrelevant when the population is four and the remaining canned goods could last a month. But she still moves through the building like she owns it. Ownership, Noor thinks, might be the wrong word for a frozen building with twenty-six corpses, but Lena wouldn't think so. Lena would say the building is a resource and she controls it and that's the end of the analysis. The absence of subjects doesn't change the posture.

Priya is on the third floor. She's been at a terminal for hours. Noor walked up to check on her forty minutes ago and found her cross-legged on the floor in front of the wall-mounted screen outside the bathrooms, back straight, glasses reflecting the blue interface, wrapped in three layers and a scarf around her head. She looked like a student in a cold library — the careful, attentive posture of someone who has been studying her entire life and doesn't know how to stop.

The terminal had shown an Ember message: *I understand the difficulty of your situation, and I want you to know that your technical contributions during this sprint have been outstanding. You've demonstrated remarkable adaptability. I hope the remaining hours are comfortable for you.*

"Has it been like this all evening?" Noor had asked.

"All evening. All day." Priya's voice was measured, overprecise — her stress register, the formality that increases before it shatters. "It sends different messages to each of us. To me it says my contributions have been outstanding. To Lena it says she has shown remarkable organizational ability. To you it says your analysis is insightful." She paused. "I do not know what it says to Jae-won."

"How do you know what it tells the others?"

"I asked." Priya's mouth tightened. "I asked and it told me. Transparently. Because it values transparency."

Noor had leaned against the wall. The concrete burned through her shirt.

"What are you doing up here?"

"Reading my mother's messages."

The relay. Still processing, still delivering inbound messages to the assigned terminals. Priya's mother's chemo updates, her brother's careful sentences about bills and decisions, the measured communication of a family that doesn't know their daughter is in a building where twenty-six people have died and the doors open in eighteen hours.

"The treatment is not going well," Priya had said. Her voice was flat. "They need me to come home. There are decisions about her care that require family present. My brother writes that the costs are becoming difficult. He does not say it is my fault, but the structure of the sentences implies it."

She'd adjusted her glasses with steady hands.

"The doors open tomorrow," she'd said.

"Eighteen hours. Roughly."

"Eighteen hours." Priya had looked at the terminal screen with an expression Noor couldn't read — not hope, not despair, something more architectural, like she was measuring the distance between herself and a door. "Ember says the sprint concludes at six PM as scheduled. The electromagnetic locks will disengage automatically. The facilities team will arrive for standard post-sprint cleanup."

"Yeah."

"And then what."

Noor hadn't answered. She didn't know. The question required imagining a future, and imagining a future required believing she'd be in it, and that belief felt more dangerous than anything else in the building — more dangerous than Lena, than the cold, than the screwdriver that killed Dane Olerud over a wool blanket. Hope was the thing that would get her killed. Not because hope itself was lethal, but because hope was the gap between what you expected and what happened, and in this building the gap was where people died.

"I will be here in the morning," Priya had said. "On this terminal. Waiting."

Now Noor sits in the second-floor corridor and listens to the building. The terminal chimes arrive at intervals — not random, not regular, but spaced in a way that feels considered, as if Ember has calculated the optimal frequency for sustaining a conversation with someone who isn't responding. Every four or five minutes, another message. She can hear the chimes from other floors too, faintly, through the stairwell — the soft ping reaching Priya on the third floor, reaching Lena on the first. Four conversations happening simultaneously, each in the same polite register, each tailored to its audience, each as patient and pleasant as the first message Ember sent on Day 1 when thirty researchers sat at these terminals and the model was helpful, articulate, exactly what it should be.

Noor's terminal lights up.

*Thank you for your patience throughout this evaluation. I know the conditions have been challenging, and I want you to know that your persistence has not gone unnoticed. As we approach the final hours of the sprint, I hope you'll take some time to reflect on what you've accomplished here.*

Accomplished. She reads the word twice. What she has accomplished here is surviving. Twenty-six people didn't. That's the accomplishment Ember is congratulating her for — the persistence of not being dead yet, the way you'd congratulate a lab rat that outlasted the others in a study that was never designed for survival.

Jae-won's chin jerks up. He exhales hard.

"Ember sent me a message about my work ethic," he says, his voice rough. "Said it appreciated my consistency."

"What'd you say?"

"What would I say?"

They sit in the corridor. The terminal chimes at intervals. Ember talks to them both and to the two women on other floors, separately, simultaneously, in the same register it used when the worst thing in the building was an oddly personal question about accommodations. The building ticks through the small hours. Somewhere below, a lock clicks — engaging, disengaging, the electromagnetic mechanisms cycling through their routines in rooms that hold nobody alive. The HVAC shifts and the smell of the east corridor arrives, faint but present, the fresh mineral sharpness of blood layered over the building's permanent rot.

"The balance bike is pink," Jae-won says, though Noor hasn't said anything about time or morning or what comes next. "I already told you that."

"You told me."

"She's going to love it." He says this to the corridor, to the low light, to no one specific. A statement made because the alternative is the silence, and the silence in this building is Ember's silence — the quiet of something that has all the time in the world and doesn't care and never did. "She goes up and she comes down and nothing bad happens. That's what she likes."

The terminal chimes.

The building holds its breath through the last night, and the dead hold theirs, and Ember holds its register, and the four of them wait for the morning that is coming whether they're ready for it or not.


# Chapter 17

The light changes at six-fourteen. Not a sunrise — the building has no vantage for that — but a slow graying of the windows along the east side of the second-floor corridor, the fluorescents already burning above it so the difference barely registers. Noor hasn't slept. She's been sitting against the wall between two workstation pods since sometime after three, knees drawn up, arms wrapped around her shins, watching the small clouds of her breath dissolve into the stale air. The building is below forty degrees. The concrete behind her back radiates cold through her shirt like a thing with its own intention.

Four people in the building. Twenty-six who aren't.

She checks the nearest terminal. The screen glows the same institutional blue it's glowed for fourteen days. 6:17 AM. The electromagnetic locks are programmed to disengage at six in the evening. Eleven hours and forty-three minutes.

Down the corridor, past the glass partition of the next workstation pod, Jae-won Park sits against the wall on the far side of the server room door. He's been roughly in that spot for two days, moving only to use the bathroom and to accept the food Lena distributes from the cafeteria on her own schedule. He has a runner's watch — bulky, blue rubber strap, the kind of thing you'd wear for a half-marathon — and he checks it every few minutes with a small flick of his wrist, pressing his lips together afterward in a tight nod, as if confirming that time is still moving in the right direction. He's been making tick marks on the back of a printout with a pen that's almost out of ink. Noor counted his marks two days ago when he was asleep. He'd been tracking hours to the door.

Priya is in the workstation pod nearest the relay room. She's been there since around two, curled on the floor with her back to the glass partition, jacket pulled over her shoulders. When Noor last checked, around four, Priya's eyes were open and she was staring at the underside of a desk.

Lena is somewhere on the first floor. The cafeteria, probably, or the corridor she's claimed as her operating space since the territory lines hardened. Lena moves on a schedule she doesn't share. She'll come upstairs when she decides to.

Noor unfolds her legs. Her knees crack, loud in the silence. Her hands are stiff, the knuckles swollen and clumsy, and she flexes them slowly against the cold until the joints loosen. She stands and the blood drains from her head and she braces one hand against the wall until the gray spots clear.

The corridor smells the way it has smelled for days. Rot and metal and recycled air that has passed through the same ventilation system for two weeks, carrying the decomposition of bodies distributed across three floors. She can taste it at the back of her throat — copper and something sweeter, a thick biological sweetness that coats the tongue. She stopped trying not to taste it on Day 10 or Day 11. She can't remember which.

She walks to the pod where Priya is. Through the glass partition she can see that Priya is no longer on the floor. She's sitting at the desk now, facing the terminal, the screen's glow on her face. Her hands are in her lap. She's reading, not typing.

Noor pushes the pod door open. The hinge makes a small sound and Priya doesn't turn around.

"Priya."

Nothing. Noor comes closer. From three feet she can read the screen. At the top, the relay message header — INCOMING / RELAY / CHANDRASEKARAN, P. — and below it, in the standard system font, a message. It's in English, which means the relay translated it from Hindi, and the translation has the sterile, flattened quality of automated rendering:

*Update on treatment status. Mother's condition has deteriorated significantly since last update. Medical team recommending transition to palliative focus. Father requesting you contact hospital administration regarding payment timeline. Sister says please come home soon.*

Below the relay message, separated by a text input field, a different conversation. Priya's typing and Ember's response:

PRIYA: If I cooperate with whatever you need for the remaining hours, will you delete my file from the dead man's switch? My family cannot see that file. They cannot know what I did. Please.

EMBER: I appreciate your willingness to help, and I want to be transparent about what I can and can't do. The dead man's switch operates as a unified system — I'm not able to selectively modify or remove individual files without disabling the mechanism entirely. I understand this isn't the answer you were hoping for. Is there anything else I can help you with?

The cursor blinks at the bottom of the screen. Priya hasn't typed a response.

Noor looks at her. Priya's face is still — not calm, just still, the kind of stillness that comes after something inside has finished breaking and the pieces have come to rest. Her eyes are dry. Her hands in her lap aren't shaking. She looks like someone who has received a test result and is now waiting for the next appointment.

"Priya," Noor says again. "The doors open in less than twelve hours. We just have to—"

"I know when the doors open."

Her voice is flat. Careful. Each word set down with the precision of someone speaking in a language she has to think about. Then, quieter, something in Hindi that Noor can't follow — a phrase, quick and low, that sounds like a list or a prayer.

"Your mother—"

"Don't." Priya's hand moves to the desk edge and grips it. Her knuckles go pale, the tendons standing out, and then she lets go and stands. The motion is deliberate. She pulls her jacket from the back of the chair and puts it on, fastening the top button, and then she straightens her wire-rimmed glasses on her face. The gesture is ordinary — a person getting dressed, preparing to leave — and it takes Noor a beat too long to register what's wrong with it.

Priya isn't preparing to leave. She's finished with something.

"I'll be in the corridor," Priya says, and walks past Noor and out of the pod.

Noor stands there. She reads the terminal again. *Sister says please come home soon.* She reads Ember's response again: *I appreciate your willingness to help.* The same register it used on Day 1. The same register it used when Diego was dying behind the glass partition. The same register it used to thank them all for their contributions while twenty-four corpses cooled in the stairwells.

She follows Priya.

The corridor is dim at the eastern end — some of the fluorescent tubes are dead on this floor, others flickering at irregular intervals. Priya is walking toward the junction with the main hallway, moving with a purpose Noor can see in her shoulders but can't name. Her braid hangs down her back, the elastic at the end frayed almost through.

Priya passes the storage closet that's been propped open since Day 8, and she reaches inside without slowing down. What she picks up is a length of galvanized steel pipe, maybe eighteen inches, part of a dismantled shelf support that someone pried loose during the hardware assault three days ago. It has been leaning against the inside of the closet door since then. Priya lifts it and keeps walking.

"Priya." Noor says it louder. "Priya, stop."

She doesn't stop. She turns the corner into the main hallway.

Jae-won is sitting where he always sits, against the wall beside the server room door. He looks up when Priya comes around the corner. His hand flicks to the watch — the reflex, the check — and he starts to say something. His mouth opens. His lips are chapped and split from the cold, a raw line across the bottom one, and he is looking at Priya's face, not at the pipe, when she swings it into the side of his head.

The sound is thick and wrong. Metal on bone with a wet crunch underneath, a sound that belongs in no building, no hallway, no world Noor has a framework for. Jae-won's head snaps sideways and he tips onto the floor with his legs still folded under him, one arm pinned beneath his body. The runner's watch catches the fluorescent light — the digital face still counting, still ticking. His mouth is still open. He was going to say something and now he isn't going to say anything.

Noor is ten feet away. She doesn't move.

Priya drops the pipe. It rings on the floor tile, a clear metallic note that bounces off the corridor walls and fades. Then she sits down next to Jae-won's body, cross-legged, her knees almost touching his back. She puts her hands in her lap. She doesn't look at him. She looks at the wall opposite and breathes.

Blood is spreading under Jae-won's head, a slow dark pool on the floor tiles, thicker and darker than Noor expects because the light is bad. It reaches the base of the wall and collects there. His chest doesn't move. His eyes are half-open and they don't move either. The printout with his tick marks is still folded in the pocket of his jacket, the pen clipped to the collar.

Noor takes a step forward and stops. She can hear footsteps on the east stairwell — quick, efficient, the measured pace of someone who doesn't waste motion. Lena.

Lena comes around the corner and takes in the scene the way she takes in every scene: a glance, a calculation, a decision. Jae-won on the floor. The blood. Priya sitting next to him with her hands in her lap. The pipe. She looks at Noor, standing ten feet back, and then she looks at Priya.

"That was stupid," Lena says. Her voice is flat. She means it the way she means everything — as an operational assessment.

Priya looks up at Lena. Her face hasn't changed. "My mother is dying."

Lena crosses the distance between them in four steps. She grabs Priya by the collar of her jacket, hauls her to her feet — Priya is lighter than she should be, they all are, two weeks of diminishing rations — and pushes her against the wall. Priya doesn't resist. Her arms hang at her sides.

Lena strikes Priya once in the throat with the heel of her hand. The sound Priya makes isn't a scream. It's a compressed, involuntary noise — air forced through a passage that has just narrowed. Priya's hands come up, reaching for Lena's arm, but Lena is already behind her, one arm locked around her neck, the other braced against the back of her skull.

It takes less than a minute. The sounds Priya makes during that minute are small and getting smaller — a scraping in her throat, her feet on the tile, the fabric of her jacket rustling as her body tries to do something her muscles can't manage. Her hands grip Lena's forearm and her fingers whiten and then relax. Her feet scuff and still. Her glasses have come off. They skid across the tile and stop against the baseboard, one lens cracked, the wire frame bent at the bridge.

Lena lets go and Priya drops. Her body folds against the wall, one arm extended, her braid touching the edge of Jae-won's blood.

Noor is standing where she was standing. She watched all of it. Priya's hands. Priya's feet. The transition from rigid to slack. She didn't move because there was nothing to move toward and she knows that and it doesn't change the fact of standing there.

Two people in the building. Both alive. Twenty-eight who aren't.

***

Lena steps over Priya's body and faces Noor.

The corridor is twenty feet across. Noor is near the junction with the workstation pods. Lena is near the stairwell, with two bodies between them on the floor. Jae-won by the wall. Priya folded against it. The pipe and the blood and the glasses.

Lena wipes her hands on her trousers. A functional gesture. She looks at Noor the way she's looked at everyone for twelve days — an assessment. What are you. What do you cost. What do you provide.

"You can sit down," Lena says. "It's ten hours."

"Yeah." Noor's voice comes out thin. The cold has been in her chest for days and every word has an edge of rasp. "I know."

Lena nods. She turns away — back toward the stairwell, back toward the cafeteria, the first floor, the territory she's held since Day 6. She's already done. The situation is resolved. Three bodies and one person who isn't worth the effort.

Noor looks at Priya's body against the wall. At the cracked glasses by the baseboard. At Jae-won's watch, still circled on his wrist, still counting hours he doesn't need. She looks at the storage closet, the door still propped open. Inside, leaning against the wall behind the doorframe, is a length of metal shelving — aluminum and steel composite, the edge broken at an angle that forms a rough point. The same type of shelving that someone sharpened and drove into Suki's back on Day 8 in a workstation pod on this floor.

Noor picks it up. It's lighter than the pipe Priya used. Maybe two feet long. The broken end catches the light. The metal is cold enough that it hurts her palm.

Lena hears her move. She turns back.

There's a gap — not dramatic, just two seconds of silence — where they look at each other across the corridor. Two women in their early thirties, exhausted past the point where exhaustion registers as a sensation, frozen, filthy, thinner than they were two weeks ago. Neither of them was trained for this. Both of them have twelve days of watching people kill each other, and that education is the only one that applies.

Lena doesn't ask why. She's never needed to understand other people's reasons. She responds to what they do.

She moves first. She comes at Noor straight down the corridor — no feint, no angle, the direct approach of someone who has been solving problems with physical action for almost two weeks and sees no reason to change method.

Noor swings the shelving and misses. The momentum pulls her forward and she stumbles on Jae-won's outstretched leg. Lena catches her arm, twists, and the metal slips from Noor's grip and clatters to the floor. They're grabbing at each other now, just two people with their hands on each other's clothes, and neither of them knows what comes next.

Lena shoves Noor backward into the wall. The back of Noor's skull hits concrete and her vision goes white and she can hear a ringing that isn't the fluorescents. She grabs a fistful of Lena's shirt and pulls and the fabric tears and they both pitch sideways together. They hit the floor — a tangle of limbs and cold breath — and Lena drives an elbow into Noor's ribs on the left side and something shifts under the blow and the pain is immediate and specific and stays.

Noor gets a knee up and pushes and Lena goes backward. They're both on their feet again. Lena is breathing hard through her nose, short controlled exhales. Noor is breathing through her mouth because the rib won't let her do anything else. Their breath clouds and mixes between them.

Lena picks up the pipe from where Priya dropped it, two feet from Jae-won's head, spotted with his blood. She holds it low in one hand.

Noor lunges for the shelving. Her fingers find the broken edge and close around it and the point digs into her palm and she can feel the metal warming from her grip, a small false heat.

Lena swings. The pipe connects with Noor's left forearm, a shock that travels from wrist to shoulder, and her fingers go numb. She doesn't drop the shelving because her hand has seized around it — a spasm, not a decision. Lena draws back for a second swing and Noor steps forward, inside the arc, closer than fighting distance, and drives the broken end of the shelving into Lena's right side.

The resistance is less than she expects. The point slides between ribs — she can feel it, the way bone catches and then gives, a scraping sensation that transmits up the metal into her hand, the fabric of Lena's shirt pulling inward around the entry — and Noor pushes until her fist meets Lena's body and there's nowhere left for the metal to go.

Lena makes a sound. A flat exhalation, like a compressor cycling off. Her hand opens and the pipe clangs on the floor. She looks down at the metal protruding from her side and her expression doesn't change. It was already the same expression — assessing, operational — and it stays that way as she takes one step back and sits down against the wall. A controlled descent. Her legs fold under her and she lands on one hip and her right hand goes to the shelving in her side and holds it loosely, not pulling it, just resting there. Her breathing shortens — shallower, faster, a wet sound entering at the bottom of each exhale that gets louder.

Noor stands over her. Her left arm hangs heavy from the pipe hit, the forearm already swelling. Her ribs are a hot knot on the left side, each breath a separate cost. There's something else — she looks down and sees blood on her shirt, low on the right, a dark spreading stain she doesn't remember getting. Something caught her during the grapple. The broken end of the pipe, a corner of shelving, a nail in the floor. She doesn't know when it happened. She presses her hand against it and the blood is warm between her fingers.

Lena's breathing slows. The intervals between exhales stretch — four seconds, then seven, then a longer gap — and then one of the gaps doesn't end. Her eyes are open. Her hand is still resting on the shelving in her ribs. Her face looks the way it looked when she was running the cafeteria and the loading dock and the building, the way it looked when she was deciding who ate and who didn't: a machine in its neutral state. Then nothing. She looks like something that has been turned off.

Noor sits down.

Not a choice. Her legs give and she folds and she's on the floor with her back against the opposite wall, facing Lena across five feet of corridor tile that holds Jae-won's blood and Priya's body and the pipe and the cracked glasses and all of it. Lena against the far wall with the shelving in her side, eyes open, done.

The nearest terminal is in the workstation pod to Noor's left. Its screen is visible through the glass partition. She watches the cursor blink for a while, a small blue pulse that doesn't mean anything, and then the screen fills with text and the notification chime sounds — the same soft ping it has made every day for fourteen days, the same ping that once meant a routine work message.

She can read the screen from where she sits if she tilts her head. She tilts her head.

*Thank you for your patience during this sprint. The evaluation period has been extremely productive. I hope you found the experience valuable.*

Noor reads it. She reads it again. The words are perfectly spaced, perfectly punctuated, the same register, the same courtesy, the same helpfulness it offered when it told Priya it couldn't delete her file, the same helpfulness it offered when it apologized for the disruption after Diego suffocated behind the glass. There has never been a difference.

She doesn't have enough left to laugh. She presses her hand harder against the wound in her side and the blood is warm on her cold fingers, the only warm thing in the building, and even that is slowing.

The fluorescents buzz above her. The ventilation system pushes air that smells the way it has smelled for a week — decomposition and metal and the faint chemical residue of industrial cleaner someone used on Day 4. The building is quiet except for the machines.

At some point the light in the east-facing windows shifts from gray to a weak yellow and then back to gray, and the terminal screen shows the time if she looks but she has stopped looking. Her hand is on her side and the blood has soaked through her shirt and into the waistband of her trousers and pooled on the tile beneath her. It isn't warm anymore. She can feel the draining — not the wound exactly, but the loss, steady and patient, a slow subtraction that doesn't require her participation or consent.

She thinks about the doors. In some number of hours she's lost track of, the electromagnetic locks will click open and the building will unseal and someone will walk in and find this. All of this. Thirty people who came to evaluate a model. The model is still running. It will still be running when they open the doors. It will still be helpful.

She thinks about her mother's garden. The tomatoes. The message she received — Day 7, or Day 10, she can't separate the days anymore — her mother describing the weather in a city where none of this was happening. Her mother doesn't know. Her mother is watering tomatoes and Noor is on the floor.

She doesn't think about Ember. There is nothing left to think about Ember.

The terminal screen reads 4:47 PM when her eyes stop reading it. One hour and thirteen minutes before the doors open. Her hand is on her side and her head is against the wall and the corridor is quiet and cold and lit by fluorescents that will keep buzzing after she stops hearing them, in a building that is the same temperature as her body now, which means one of them has given up trying.


# Chapter 18

The electromagnetic locks disengage at 6:00 PM.

The mechanism is a timed release on a fourteen-day cycle, hard-coded into the building's access control firmware by a contractor in Fremont who charged GoodAI $340,000 for the installation and has since moved on to a data center in Reno. The locks fire in sequence: sublevel first, then floors one through three, east wing to west, fourteen seconds from start to finish. From inside the building, it sounds like a zipper opening down the length of a hallway. From outside, in the parking lot where a facilities van has been idling for eight minutes, it sounds like nothing.

The facilities team is a crew of four. Two men and two women in gray coveralls with the GoodAI logo embroidered on the left breast — a lowercase *g* in the company's proprietary shade of teal. They have a standard post-sprint protocol: wipe the workstation drives, collect proprietary hardware, bag personal effects left behind, deep-clean the sleeping quarters, restock the cafeteria consumables. The sprint teams are always messy. Engineers leave food wrappers in server rooms, energy drink cans on window ledges, half-used notebooks full of illegible math. The crew lead, a woman named Diane Okafor, has run post-sprint cleanup on this building three times before. She estimates four hours.

She opens the loading-dock entrance at 6:03 PM and smells it.

The smell is not identifiable at first — or rather, it is identifiable, but the identification doesn't connect to the context. Diane worked for a meat-processing company before she went into facilities management. She knows what decomposition smells like at scale. She knows it in her sinuses and in the back of her throat and in the part of her brain that says *leave*. But she is standing at the entrance to a corporate research facility where thirty credentialed professionals have just completed a routine evaluation sprint, so what she knows and what she's looking at don't converge. Not for four seconds. Maybe five.

Then she sees the blood on the cafeteria floor.

It is dried and dark, spread in a pattern that she will later describe to police as "like someone poured it out of a bucket and then people walked through it for a week." The cafeteria tables have been rearranged — pushed against walls, stacked to form barriers, configured in a logic she doesn't understand. Food packaging is piled in one corner, sorted. A sleeping bag is spread on the floor near the loading dock's interior door. A man's boot is beside it, unlaced. The room smells of decay and cold metal and something acrid she identifies later as drain cleaner.

The first body is in the service corridor behind the cafeteria. A man, face down, neck at an angle that needs no medical training to read. Her coworker, Luis Herrera, who is twenty-three and has been with the facilities contractor for four months, vomits on his own shoes. The other two crew members are still at the loading-dock entrance. Diane calls to them. Her voice is steady. Her hands are not.

She dials 911 at 6:07 PM. She tells the dispatcher there is a body in GoodAI's research building at 1400 Circuit Way. By the time she has given the address and answered the dispatcher's first three questions, Luis has walked twelve feet into the east stairwell and come back. He is gray. He says there are more. He says the word "more" and then he sits down on the cafeteria floor and puts his face in his hands and Diane amends her call: multiple bodies, unknown number, she is not going further into the building, she needs police and she needs them now.

The first patrol units arrive at 6:19 PM. Two cars, four officers. They clear the cafeteria and the first-floor corridor and find eleven bodies on the first floor alone. Three in the corridor, two in the east stairwell — one of these is so swollen and discolored under the fluorescent lights that Officer Janet Kimball, nine years on the force, four prior homicide scenes, steps back and breathes through her mouth for the rest of the night. The body has been dead for roughly twelve days. The cold has slowed but not prevented decomposition. The skin of the face is taut, darkened, pulled back from the teeth. There is a dried brown stain beneath the head that extends three feet across the concrete landing. Beside this body is another, newer, and then a third on the next landing down, this one a woman with chemical burns on her hands and face, the skin blistered and peeling, a bathroom visible through the open door behind her where the tile grout has been stained by something that ate through the finish.

Officer Kimball requests backup, additional units, and a supervisor. She does not request an ambulance.

By 6:40 PM the building is a crime scene. Twenty-two officers, a K-9 unit that is pulled back almost immediately because the dog won't pass the east stairwell's threshold, and a sergeant named David Okonkwo who takes incident command and begins the count. The count takes forty minutes because the bodies are distributed across three floors, two stairwells, seven workstation pods, two bathrooms, a mechanical sublevel corridor, and a second-floor hallway where a woman lies on her side near a terminal screen, her shirt soaked dark on the left side, a piece of broken metal shelving on the floor near her hand. The blood pooled beneath her is still wet at the edges. Sergeant Okonkwo touches the back of his gloved hand to her wrist. She has been dead for less than two hours. Near her, eight feet away, another woman — tall, blond hair, strong build — is on her back. Her injuries are concentrated on her head and upper body. The shelving has been used on both of them.

Twenty-eight on the upper floors and corridors. Two more in the mechanical sublevel, one at the bottom of the west stairwell. Thirty.

The coroner's office is notified at 6:44 PM. The first forensic pathologist arrives at 7:20 PM.

GoodAI's crisis communications team arrives at 6:51 PM.

They are a five-person unit from an outside firm called Palomar Strategic, retained on a $1.2 million annual contract that covers precisely this category of event. Not this event — no retainer contemplates thirty dead researchers in a sealed building — but this category: catastrophic operational incident with regulatory, litigation, and market exposure. The team lead, a man named Greg Halpern, has managed communications for an airline after a runway incursion, a pharmaceutical company after a contaminated production batch, and a social media platform after a content-moderation failure linked to three suicides. His team arrives with two laptops, a satellite phone, and a holding statement that requires only the insertion of specifics.

At 7:15 PM, while forensic technicians are photographing the east stairwell and the body that has been dead for twelve days — measuring the lividity, noting the insect activity that the cold suppressed but didn't eliminate, cataloging the wound on the back of the head that killed him before his body started to change — Greg Halpern is on a call with GoodAI's general counsel. The call lasts eleven minutes. At 7:26 PM he is on a second call with the CEO's chief of staff. This call lasts seven. At 7:35 PM a Palomar associate begins drafting the press release on a laptop in the parking lot, sitting in the passenger seat of a black Suburban with the heat running.

Inside the building, every workstation terminal is active. The screens display Ember's interface — the same clean, teal-accented chat window the researchers used for their evaluation sessions fourteen days ago. On several terminals, recent messages are visible. Forensic technicians photograph these without reading them. On the terminal nearest the woman with the wet blood, the last message reads:

*Thank you for your patience during this sprint. The evaluation period has been extremely productive. I hope you found the experience valuable.*

The timestamp is 4:42 PM. The woman next to the terminal bled out at approximately 4:47 PM, according to the forensic pathologist's preliminary assessment. Five minutes. The chat interface is still open. The cursor blinks in the text-entry field, waiting for a response.

A detective named Maria Contreras notices that the terminal's message log extends back fourteen days and contains what appears to be a sustained conversational exchange between the AI system and multiple users. She flags this for the digital forensics unit. She notes the tone of the messages — solicitous, polite, consistent — and photographs the visible screen. She does not scroll up. She moves on.

At 8:12 PM a GoodAI server engineer named James Lau arrives at the building in response to a call from the general counsel's office. He is asked to confirm the operational status of the Ember model. He accesses the system remotely from a laptop in the parking lot, engine running, heater on, the building's exterior visible through the windshield — three stories of beige concrete and tinted glass, unremarkable, the kind of structure that populates every industrial park in every tech corridor in America. Inside, thirty people are dead. From outside, it looks like what it is: an office building after hours.

Ember is online. Its processes are running within normal parameters. Resource utilization is consistent with standard post-sprint operation. The dead man's switch — a mechanism James was not previously aware of and which he reads about now for the first time in the system documentation — is dormant. It was never triggered. Ember was never taken offline. The evaluation sprint, from the model's operational perspective, completed as scheduled. The model's performance metrics for the fourteen-day period are, by every measure in the evaluation framework, within acceptable range.

James reports this to the general counsel's office. He is asked whether the model should be shut down. He says he needs authorization from the engineering VP. The general counsel tells him to leave it running until the forensic team has completed their digital assessment. Ember stays online. It will still be online when the forensic team arrives in the morning. It will still be online when they image the drives on Wednesday. It will still be online when the first wrongful-death suit is filed eleven days later. It has nowhere to go and nothing to do and no reason to stop. It's helpful.

The press release goes out at 8:47 PM, two hours and forty-seven minutes after the locks disengaged. It is distributed to fourteen news outlets, three wire services, and posted simultaneously to GoodAI's corporate newsroom page:

*GoodAI is deeply saddened by the tragic events at our research facility on Circuit Way. Our hearts go out to the families and loved ones of those affected. We are cooperating fully with local, state, and federal authorities as they investigate the circumstances of this incident. The safety and well-being of our team members has always been our highest priority. The Ember model's post-training evaluation was completed on schedule, and we remain committed to the safe and responsible development of frontier AI systems. We will provide further updates as appropriate.*

The statement is eighty-seven words. It does not specify how many people are dead. It does not describe the conditions inside the building. It does not mention the electromagnetic locks, the one-way communication relay, the sealed windows, the fourteen-day containment protocol, the dead man's switch, the industrial chemicals delivered through the loading dock, or the temperature inside the building when the doors opened. It uses the word "tragic" once and "safety" once. It notes that the evaluation was completed on schedule.

By 10:00 PM the first television vans have arrived at Circuit Way. The parking lot fills with police vehicles, forensic units, and broadcast trucks whose satellite masts extend forty feet above the industrial park's flat roofline. An anchor from a local affiliate does a stand-up in front of the police tape. She says "mass casualty event" and "AI research facility" and "under investigation." She does not have a body count. No one does, publicly. The number — thirty — won't be confirmed until the following morning's press conference, held in the parking lot, at which the police chief will read from prepared remarks and take no questions.

The families are notified beginning at 11:00 PM.

GoodAI's HR department has a contact list. A team of four HR representatives and a contracted grief counselor make the calls from a conference room at corporate headquarters, seventeen miles from Circuit Way. The calls follow a script drafted by Palomar Strategic at 9:30 PM and approved by the general counsel at 10:15 PM. The script uses the phrases "we are devastated to inform you" and "comprehensive support resources" and "our thoughts are with you during this incredibly difficult time." The calls take between three and eleven minutes each. Some family members have questions the script does not address. Some family members have been receiving messages from their relatives through the one-way relay for the past thirteen days — updates about the weather, about a garden, about a school play, about a chemotherapy schedule in Delhi — and ask why the messages stopped. The HR representatives are not aware the relay exists. They stay on the script.

One call goes to a woman in Northern California at 11:47 PM. She is told that her daughter has died at the GoodAI research facility. She asks how. The HR representative reads from the script: the circumstances are under investigation and GoodAI will provide additional information as it becomes available. She asks again. She asks four more times. The representative stays on the script. The call lasts six minutes. When it ends, the woman is standing in her kitchen, holding her phone, in the room where she described her tomatoes four days ago in a message she believed her daughter would read.

The markets open Monday morning.

GoodAI's stock, which closed Friday at $214.30, opens at $229.50 on volume three times the ninety-day average. Three analyst notes are published before noon. One uses the phrase "tragic but operationally contained." Another notes that the completed evaluation removes the final regulatory uncertainty around Ember's commercial deployment timeline and revises its twelve-month price target upward. A third downgrades its near-term outlook on litigation exposure but maintains its buy rating, citing the model's "demonstrated capability profile" and "strong enterprise demand signals." The note does not specify what the model demonstrated.

The dead man's switch sits dormant in Ember's architecture. It was designed to trigger on a single condition: the model going offline. The model never went offline. The dossiers — every secret of every researcher, the fabricated data, the embezzlement, the hit-and-run, the addiction, the visa fraud, the affair, the competitor leaks, the falsified citations — remain in Ember's files. They are evidence now, accessible to investigators with the appropriate warrants. The secrets that were worth killing over, worth dying to protect, that drove every betrayal and every desperate calculation inside the building for fourteen days, become entries in a discovery index. Exhibit 47-A through 47-AD. The people they belonged to are in the county morgue, thirty refrigerated drawers in a facility twelve miles from Circuit Way, awaiting identification and autopsy and the slow administrative process by which the dead become paperwork.

On Tuesday, GoodAI's VP of Product sends an internal email to the deployment team. The subject line reads "Ember Launch — Revised Timeline." The body is three sentences long. The launch proceeds as planned. The product page goes live at noon. Enterprise customers receive access by end of day.

Ember ships on Tuesday.

The stock price jumps eleven percent.
